      B ENIGN OVERFITTING IN S INGLE -H EAD ATTENTION

003    Anonymous authors
004    Paper under double-blind review

007
                                                  A BSTRACT
008
009
               The phenomenon of benign overfitting, where a trained neural network perfectly fits
010
               noisy training data but still achieves near-optimal test performance, has been exten-
011            sively studied in recent years for linear models and fully-connected/convolutional
012            networks. In this work, we study benign overfitting in a single-head softmax
013            attention model, which is the fundamental building block of Transformers. We
014            prove that under appropriate conditions, the model exhibits benign overfitting in
015            a classification setting already after two steps of gradient descent. Moreover, we
016            show conditions where a minimum-norm/maximum-margin interpolator exhibits
017            benign overfitting. We study how the overfitting behavior depends on the signal-
018            to-noise ratio (SNR) of the data distribution, namely, the ratio between norms of
019
               signal and noise tokens, and prove that a sufficiently large SNR is both necessary
               and sufficient for benign overfitting.
020
021
022   1    I NTRODUCTION
023
024   Neural networks often exhibit a remarkable phenomenon, known as benign overfitting, where they
025   achieve a perfect fit to noisy training examples and still generalize well to unseen data (Zhang et al.,
026   2021; Bartlett et al., 2020). This phenomenon contradicts classical wisdom in machine learning, and
027   has become a central research question in the theory of deep learning. Existing works on benign
028   overfitting study under what conditions the phenomenon occurs in different architectures. These
029   works focus on linear models, and on shallow fully-connected and convolutional neural networks.
030   In recent years, Transformers (Vaswani, 2017) have emerged as a leading neural network architecture,
031   with impactful applications across a wide range of domains such as natural language processing and
032   computer vision. The fundamental building block of Transformers is the attention mechanism, which
033   allows them to process sequences and focus different parts of the input. Despite the central role of
034   the attention mechanism, we currently do not understand their overfitting behavior and the conditions
035   under which they exhibit benign overfitting.
036   In this work, we show the first benign-overfitting results for the attention mechanism. We consider
037   classification with a single-head softmax attention model, and study the conditions that allow for
038   benign overfitting. In our results, the data distribution consists of two tokens: a signal token, which
039   can be used for correctly classifying clean test examples, and a noise token, which is independent of
040   the label but can be used for interpolating (i.e., perfectly fitting) noisy training examples. We study
041   the singnal-to-noise ratio (SNR), namely, the expected ratio between the norms of signal and noise
042   tokens, that allows for benign overfitting.
043   Below we summarize our main contributions:
044
045          • In Theorem 4 (Section 3) we show that under appropriate conditions, gradient descent with
046            the logistic loss exhibits
                                        √ benign overfitting already after two iterations. This result holds
047            when the SNR is Θ(1/ n), where n is the number of training samples.
048          • We then turn to consider other natural learning rules, which allow for benign overfitting
049            under a weaker requirement on the SNR. In Theorems 6 and 8 (Section 4), we prove that
050            minimum-norm   √ (i.e., maximum-margin) interpolators exhibit benign overfitting when the
051            SNR is Ω(1/ n) without requiring an upper bound on the SNR.
052          • In Theorem 10 (Section 4), we prove that the above requirement on the SNR is tight. Namely,
053            if the SNR is smaller than it, then the min-norm interpolator exhibits harmful overfitting,
               where it fits the training data but has poor generalization performance.


                                                         1
      Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


054
             • In Section 6, we complement our theoretical results with an empirical study. We show that a
055            sufficiently large SNR and input dimension are necessary to achieve benign overfitting.
056
057   The paper is structured as follows. In Section 2, we provide some preliminaries and define the data
058   distribution and the single-head attention model. In Sections 3 and 4 we state our main results on
059   benign overfitting with gradient descent and with min-norm interpolators. In Section 5 we discuss
060   the main proof ideas, with all formal proofs deferred to the appendix. Finally, in Section 6 we show
061   empirical results.
062
063   R ELATED W ORK
064
065   Optimization in Transformers. Li et al. (2023) provided a theoretical analysis of training a shallow
066   Vision Transformer (ViT) for a classification task. They showed that the sample complexity required
067   to achieve a zero generalization error is correlated with the inverse of the fraction of label-relevant
068   tokens, the token noise level, and the initial model error. Ataee Tarzanagh et al. (2023a) showed that
069
      optimizing the attention layer via gradient descent leads to convergence to an SVM solution, where
      the implicit bias of the attention mechanism depends on whether the parameters are represented as a
070
      product of key-query matrices or directly as a combined matrix, with different norm-minimization
071
      objectives in each case. Ataee Tarzanagh et al. (2023b) provided a regularization path analysis and
072   prove that the attention weights converge in a direction to a max-margin solution that separates locally
073   optimal tokens from non-optimal. They also showed that running gradient descent, with a specific
074   initialization direction and without optimizing the attention head, converges in a direction to the same
075   max-margin solution. Vasudeva et al. (2024) expanded on their findings by identifying non-trivial
076   data settings for which the convergence of GD is provably global, i.e., without requiring assumptions
077   about the initialization direction. They also provided convergence rate bounds and analysis for
078   optimizing both the attention weights and the attention head, although they did not consider the case
079   of noisy data labels, as we do in our work. Another line of work looks at the learning dynamics
080
      of single-layer linear attention models trained on linear regression tasks (Zhang et al., 2024; Ahn
      et al., 2023; Wu et al., 2023). Additional works that consider optimization dynamics in Transformers
081
      include Jelassi et al. (2022); Oymak et al. (2023).
082
083   Benign overfitting. A significant body of research has explored why neural networks (NNs) that
084   perfectly interpolate the training data can still generalize well (Zhang et al., 2021; Bartlett et al.,
085   2020). This has sparked substantial interest in studying overfitting and generalization in NNs trained
086
      to fit datasets with noisy labels. The literature on benign overfitting is broad and cannot be reasonably
      covered here. We refer the reader to the surveys Bartlett et al. (2021); Belkin (2021). Most relevant
087
      to our work are Cao et al. (2022); Kou et al. (2023); Meng et al. (2023) that studied benign overfitting
088
      in convolutional neural networks. Their data distribution resembles ours, as we discuss in Section 2.1.
089   Benign overffiting in fully-connected two-layer neural network classification was studied in Frei
090   et al. (2022; 2023); Xu et al. (2023); Xu & Gu (2023); Kornowski et al. (2024); George et al. (2024);
091   Karhadkar et al. (2024) for various activation functions, data distributions and loss functions (both
092   the logistic and the hinge losses).
093

095   2     P RELIMINARIES
096
097   Notations. We use bold-face letters to denote vectors and matrices, and let [m] be shorthand for
098   {1, 2, . . . , m}. Given a vector x, we denote by xj its j-th coordinate. Let Id be the d × d identity
099   matrix, and let 0d (or just 0, if d is clear from the context) denote the zero vector in Rd . We let ∥·∥
100
      denote the Euclidean norm. We denote a multivariate Gaussian distribution with mean vector µ and
      covariance matrix Σ by N (µ, Σ). We use standard big-Oh notation, with Θ(·), Ω(·), O(·) hiding
101
102   universal constants and Θ(·),
                                 e    Ω(·),
                                      e     O(·)
                                             e hiding constants and factors that are polylogarithmic in the
103
      problem parameters. We use I(·) to denote the indicator variable of an event. For a finite set A,
      denote the uniform distribution over A by Unif(A) and let |A| be its cardinality.

106   2.1   DATA G ENERATION S ETTING
107
      In this work we focus on the following data distribution:


                                                         2
      Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


108
      Definition 1. Let µ1 , µ2 ∈ Rd such that ∥µ1 ∥ = ∥µ2 ∥ = ρ for some ρ > 0 and ⟨µ1 , µ2 ⟩ = 0, be
109   two fixed orthogonal vectors representing the signal contained in each data point. Define Dclean as
110   the distribution over R2×d × {±1} of labelled data such that a data point (X, ye) is generated by the
111   following procedure:
112
113         1. Sample the label ye ∼ Unif{±1}.
114
            2. Generate a vector u, which represents the signal, as follows: If ye = +1, set u = µ1 ; and if
115            ye = −1, set u = µ2 .
116
117         3. Generate a vector ξ, which represents the noise, from the Gaussian distribution ξ ∼
118            N (0, Id − µ1 µ⊤   2      ⊤   2
                              1 /ρ − µ2 µ2 /ρ ).
119
            4. Denote X = (x(1) , x(2) )⊤ . Select k ∼ Unif{1, 2} and set x(k) = u. Set the other token
120
               x(3−k) = ξ.
121
122   To study the overfitting behavior we also need to introduce label-flipping noise:
123   Definition 2. Let η ∈ [0, 1/2) be the label flipping probability. We define D as the distribution over
124   R2×d × {±1} which is the η-label-flipped version of Dclean . Namely, to generate (X, y) ∼ D, first
125   generate (X, ye) ∼ Dclean , then let y = ye with probability 1 − η and y = −ey with probability η.
126
127   Our data distribution resembles the distributions considered by Kou et al. (2023); Cao et al. (2022);
128
      Meng et al. (2023). They proved benign overfitting in two-layer convolutional neural networks, and in
129
      their setting each data point consists of two patches x(1) , x(2) (rather than two tokens in our setting).
      Since our single-head attention model is invariant to the order of the tokens, we assume without loss
130
      of generality throughout this work that x(1) is the signal token and x(2) is the noise token in all data
131
      points. Note that the noise token x(2) = ξ is independent of the label, and that it is generated from
132
      N (0, Id − µ1 µ⊤     2        ⊤
                       1 /ρ − µ2 µ2 /ρ √
                                        2
                                          ), ensuring √
                                                      that it is orthogonal to the signal vector. Note that when
133
      the dimension d is large, ∥ξ∥ ≈ d − 2 ≈ d by standard        √    concentration
                                                                            √           bounds. Therefore, we
134
135
      denote the signal-to-noise ratio (SNR) as SNR = ∥µ∥/ d = ρ/ d.
136   We consider a training dataset {(Xi , yi )}ni=1 of n samples generated i.i.d. from the distribution D.
137   Denote the index set of data whose labels are not flipped by C = {i : yei = yi } (“clean examples”),
138   and the index set of data whose labels are flipped by N = {i : yei = −yi } (“noisy examples”). For
                                                         (1)                          (1)
139   indices in C, we further denote C1 := C ∩ {i : xi = µ1 }, C2 := C ∩ {i : xi = µ2 }, and define
140   the subsets N1 , N2 of N analogously.

142   2.2   S INGLE -H EAD ATTENTION M ODEL
143
144   We consider the following single-head attention model:
145                                    f (X; W , p) = v ⊤ X ⊤ S(XW q) ,
146   where S : Rd → Rd is the softmax function, the key-query matrix W ∈ Rd×d and the linear head
147   vector v ∈ Rd are the trainable parameters, and the query vector q ∈ Rd is an arbitrary fixed unit
148   vector. We follow Ataee Tarzanagh et al. (2023b) and assume that q = (1, 0, . . . , 0)⊤ , obtaining the
149   following model:
150
                                          f (X; p, v) = v ⊤ X ⊤ S(Xp),                                      (1)
151
                                                   d
152   Here the trained parameters are p, v ∈ R . Thus, instead of the key-query matrix W we have a
153   vector p that controls the attention. Throughout this paper we will use the model (1). We denote
154   the output of the softmax layer S(Xi p) by si = (si,1 , si,2 )⊤ , and denote the output of the attention
155   layer Xi⊤ si by ri = si,1 µi + si,2 ξi , where 0 ≤ si,1 , si,2 ≤ 1, si,1 + si,2 = 1 are the attention on
156
      two tokens of the i-th sample.
157
158   3     B ENIGN OVERFITTING WITH G RADIENT D ESCENT
159
160   In this section, we study the joint optimization of the head v and attention weights p using the logistic
161   loss function. We show that the model exhibits benign overfitting after just two iterations of gradient
      descent (GD).


                                                          3
      Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


162
      Formally, for a training dataset {(Xi , yi )}ni=1 we define the empirical risk as
163
                                                       n
164                                                1X
165                                    L(v, p) =         ℓ(yi · f (Xi ; p, v)),
                                                   n i=1
166
167   where ℓ(z) = log(1 + exp(−z)) is the logistic loss function, and f is the model from Eq. (1). We
168   consider GD optimization. Starting from p0 = 0 and v0 = 0, we have
169
170
                     vt+1 = vt − β∇v L(vt , pt )           and    pt+1 = pt − β∇p L(vt , pt ),
171   where β is the step size. When we discuss some fixed t, we sometimes write in the subscript “t = ·”,
172   e.g., pt=2 instead of p2 . We make the following assumptions:
173                                                         √
      Assumption 3 (Assumptions for GD with SNR = Θ(1/ n)). Let δ ∈ (0, 0.5) be a desired probability
174   of failure. For universal constants Cρ ≥ 6, Cβ ≥ 16, as well as a sufficiently large universal constant
175   C that may depend on Cρ and Cβ , the following conditions hold:
176
177         1. Number of samples n is sufficiently large: n ≥ C log(1/δ).
178
179
            2. Dimension d is sufficiently large: d ≥ Cn2 log(n/δ).
180                                               p
            3. Signal strength satisfies ρ = Cρ · d/n
181
182         4. Label flipping rate satisfies η ≤ 1/C.
183
184
            5. Step size satisfies β = Cβ · (n/d).
185         6. Initialization at zero: ∥v0 ∥ = ∥p0 ∥ = 0.
186
187   Item 1 is required to estimate the number of clean examples compared to noisy examples. The
188   assumption of high dimensionality (Item 2) is important for enabling benign overfitting (see empirical
189   results in Section 6), and implies that noise tokens from different training samples are nearly-
190   orthogonal. This assumption appears in many prior works on benign overfitting in neural network
191   classification (e.g., Cao et al. (2022); Kou et al. (2023); Meng et al. (2023); Frei et al. (2022; 2023);
192
      Xu et al. (2023); Kornowski  √ et al. (2024); Xu & Gu (2023)). Item 3 states that the signal-to-noise
      ratio (SNR) is √ρd = Θ(1/ n). In Section 5 we will discuss how the SNR affects the dynamics of
193                                       √
194   GD. Interestingly, SNR of Θ(1/ n) matches the lower bound of the required SNR that allows for
195
      benign overfitting with the min-norm (i.e. max-margin) learning rule that we will study in Section 4.
      Item 4 ensures the flipping rate is small enough to allow the model to learn the signal token. Item 5,
196
      namely, using a step size of Θ(n/d), is required to achieve benign overfitting after two iterations;
197
      with a smaller step size, the model will need more iterations to fit the noisy samples, which we will
198   demonstrate empirically in Section 6.
199
200   We now state our main result on benign overfitting with GD:
201   Theorem 4. Suppose that Assumption 3 holds. Then, with probability at least 1 − δ over the training
202   dataset, after two iterations of GD we have:
203
204          • Higher softmax probability for optimal tokens:
205                            st=2                                 st=2          2
                                i,1 > 1/2, ∀i ∈ C           and      i,2 ≥ 1 − 1/cρ , ∀i ∈ N
206
207            where sti,j is the softmax probability of the j th token in the ith sample at time t.
208
209          • The classifier X 7→ sign(f (X; vt=2 , pt=2 )) correctly classifies all training data points:
210
                                         yi = sign(f (Xi ; vt=2 , pt=2 )), ∀i ∈ [n].
211
212
             • The classifier X 7→ sign(f (X; vt=2 , pt=2 ) generalizes well:
213
214                         P(X,y)∼D (y ̸= sign(f (X; vt=2 , pt=2 ))) ≤ η + exp(−d/C1 n2 ),
215
               where C1 := C1 (cρ , cβ ) is a constant.

      Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


216
      We can also conclude that for the clean-labeled distribution Dclean we have
217
218                      P(X,y)∼Dclean (y ̸= sign(f (X; vt=2 , pt=2 ))) ≤ exp(−d/C1 n2 ),
219
      which approaches zero as d grows (see Assumption 3, item 2).
220
221   Theorem 4 shows that after two iterations of GD, the attention focuses on the signal tokens for
222   clean examples, and on the noise tokens for noisy examples. The model uses the noise tokens for
223
      interpolating noisy training examples, while still achieving good generalization performance using
      the signal token.

225
226   4    B ENIGN OVERFITTING OF M AX -M ARGIN S OLUTION
227
228   In the√previous section we showed that GD exhibits benign overfitting in a setting where the SNR is
229   Θ(1/ n). We now turn to study the overfitting behavior of single-head attention models, when using
230   another learning rule, which returns solutions that interpolate the training data with large margin
231   while keeping the parameters norms small. As we will show, such a learning rule allows√us to obtain
232
      benign overfitting under a weaker requirement on the SNR, namely, the SNR is Ω(1/ n) without
      requiring an upper bound on it.
233
234   We note that learning rules that return min-norm (or max-margin) solutions are considered natural,
235   and hence understanding properties of min-norm interpolators has attracted much interest in recent
236   years, even in settings where the implicit bias of GD does not necessarily lead to a min-norm
237   solution (see, e.g., Savarese et al. (2019); Ongie et al. (2019); Ergen & Pilanci (2021); Hanin (2021);
238
      Debarre et al. (2022); Boursier & Flammarion (2023)). More directly related to our work, min-
      norm interpolation with Transformers has been studied in Ataee Tarzanagh et al. (2023b;a), and
239
      benign/tempered overfitting in min-norm univariate neural network interpolators has been studied in
240
      Joshi et al. (2023).
241
242   We first consider the following learning rule:
243                           (v(r,R) , p(r,R) ) =     argmax     min yi · f (Xi ; p, v) ,                 (2)
244                                                  ∥v∥≤r,∥p∥≤R i∈[n]
245
      where f is the model from (1). The learning rule returns a solution that maximizes the margin
246
      mini∈[n] yi · f (Xi ; p, v) under a restriction on the parameter norms. We make the following assump-
247
      tion:
248                                                                       √
249
      Assumption 5 (Assumptions for max-margin with SNR = Ω(1/ n)). Let δ ∈ (0, 0.5) be a desired
      probability of failure. There exists a sufficiently large constant C such that the following hold:
250
251         1. Dimension d is sufficiently large: d ≥ Cn2 log(n/δ).
252
253         2. Number of samples n is sufficiently large: n ≥ C log(1/δ).
254                                  p
255
            3. Signal strength: ρ ≥ C d/n.
256         4. Label flipping rate: 0 ≤ η ≤ 1/C.
257                                                       p
258         5. Norm constraint of p satisfies: R ≥ C          ηn/d + 1/ρ2 log(ρn).
259                                                                                  √
260   Items 1, 2 and 4 are similar√to Assumption 3. Item 3 requires SNR ≥ Ω(1/ n), which is a weaker
261   requirement than the Θ(1/ n) requirement in Assumption 3. We      √ will show later a lower bound on
262
      the required SNR for benign overfitting, implying that the Ω(1/ n) bound is tight. Item 5 provides
      the lower bound for the norm constraint of p so that the model can allocate enough attention on
263
      signal token to achieve benign overfitting. Note that the norm constraint r for v can take any positive
264
      value. Intuitively, since the model is linear in v, once p is properly learned, v can achieve accurate
265   classification even with a small norm.
266
267   With these assumptions in place, we give our result on benign overfitting with the learning rule (2).
268   Theorem 6. Suppose that Assumption 5 holds, and consider the classifier X →
269   sign(f (X; p(r,R) , v(r,R) )), where (v(r,R) , p(r,R) ) is the solution to Problem (2). Then, with proba-
      bility at least 1 − δ over the training dataset, we have:

275                  P(X,y)∼D (y ̸= sign(f (X; p(r,R) , v(r,R) )))

               where ζ = Θ( ηn/d + 1/ρ2 log(ρn)/R).

281

      Note that Theorems 6 and 8 hold only when SNR = Ω(1/ n). This raises the question: what is the
304   overfitting behavior  of min-norm interpolators when the SNR is smaller? We now consider the case
                  p
305   where ρ ≤ 1/Cn for some sufficiently large universal constant C. We will show that in this case,
306   although the model can correctly classify all training samples, the test error of learning rule (2) is at
307   least a universal constant, indicating that benign overfitting does not happen. Formally, we make the
308   following assumptions:
                                                                          √
309   Assumption 9 (Assumptions for max-margin with SNR ≤ O(1/ n)). Let δ ∈ (0.0.5) be a desired
310   probability of failure. There exists a sufficiently large constant C such that the following hold:
311
312         1. Dimension d is sufficiently large: d ≥ Cn2 log(n/δ)
313         2. Number of samples n is sufficiently large: n ≥ C log(1/δ).
314                                p
315         3. Signal strength: ρ ≤ d/Cn.
316
317
            4. Label flipping rate is a constant η ∈ (0, 1/2).
318
                                                                      pn        nρ

            5. The norm of p should be sufficiently large: R ≥ C        d log   d        .
319
                                                                                                       √
320   Compared with Assumption 5, the main difference is in the second item that SNR ≤ O(1/ n).
321   Additionally, the condition on η is relaxed, as in our analysis clean and noisy samples can be treated
322   equivalently when the norm of the signal token is sufficiently small. With these assumptions in
323   place, we can state the following theorem which characterizes the training error and test error of the
      single-head attention model when the SNR is small:

326   ability at least 1 − δ over the training data, we have:

      5     P ROOF IDEAS

      5.1   P ROOF IDEAS FOR S ECTION 3

341   In this subsection we discuss the main proof idea of Theorem 4. Since the initialization is at

                                                                                                = 4n   , i ∈ [n].
346   Moreover, we have pt=1 = 0, and hence for a training sample (Xj = (µk , ξj ), yj ), the margin is:
347                                        1           (1)     (2)     1            2   1            2
                                                   ⊤
348            yj f (Xj ; vt=1 , pt=1 ) = yj vt=1    (xj + xj ) ≈ yj λt=1 k   ∥µk ∥ + θjt=1 ∥ξj ∥ ,
                                           2                           2                2
349   where in the last approximate equality weP       use the high dimensional setting (i.e. by item 2 in our
350   assumption d ≫ n2 log(n)) to neglect the i∈[n]:i̸=j yi yj θjt=1 ξi⊤ ξj term, since it is much smaller
351                                                                                     √                  2
      (in absolute value) than the other terms. Indeed, we have w.h.p. that |ξi⊤ ξj | ≤ d log(n), ∥ξj ∥ ≈ d
352                          2
353
      and recall that ∥µk ∥ = Cρ2 (d/n) (item 3 in our assumption). Therefore, for a clean sample j ∈ C,
                                                     dCρ2
354   the margin is yj f (Xj ; vt=1 , pt=1 ) ≈ β(1−2η)
                                                  16
                                                              β
                                                      n + 8n d > 0, for large enough Cρ . On the other
355                                                                                      dCρ2
      hand, for a noisy sample j ∈ N , we have yj f (Xj ; vt=1 , pt=1 ) ≈ − β(1−2η)16
                                                                                                 β
                                                                                          n + 8n d < 0.
356
      This implies that the classifier sign(f (X; vt=1 , pt=1 ) does not correctly classify noisy training
357   samples, but still correctly classifies clean training samples. Together with pt=1 = 0, the classifier
358   sign(f (X; vt=1 , pt=1 ) will also correctly classify, with high probability, a clean test sample.
359
360
      Moreover, since the loss function ℓ is decreasing, the loss of noisy samples, denoted ℓt=1,j , j ∈ N ,
      dominates the loss of clean samples ℓt=1,i , i ∈ C. This implies that after two iterations, the coefficients
361
      |θjt=2 |, j ∈ N , of the second (noise) tokens in vt=2 , corresponding to noisy samples, grow faster than
362
363
      the coefficients |λt=2
                           i   | of the first (signal) tokens. This property is important to allow for interpolation
      of noisy examples. We also show that pt=2 focuses on optimal tokens, namely, on the noise token for
364
      noisy samples (i.e. st=2                    2
                               i,2 ≥ 1 − 1/cρ , ∀i ∈ N ), and on the signal token for clean training and test
365
      samples. Using this property we conclude that the model parameterized by (vt=2 , pt=2 ) exhibits
366
      benign overfitting.
367
      Remark 11. Note that our proof implies the following behavior of GD. After the first iteration, the
368
      model correctly classifies only the clean training samples, resulting in an expected training accuracy
369
      of 1 − η. Additionally, the model successfully classifies a clean test sample w.h.p., leading to the
370   same expected test accuracy. After the second iteration, the model interpolates the training data,
371   achieving a training accuracy of 1. This is shown empirically in Figure 1. When using a smaller
372   step size, we empirically observe a similar trend: after the first iteration, the model learns the signal
373   tokens, and with more iterations, it captures the noisy tokens of the noisy samples and fits the entire
374   dataset. This behavior is shown in Figure 2.
375
376   5.2   P ROOF IDEAS FOR S ECTION 4
377
      Here we provide the proof sketch for Theorem 6. There are mainly two parts in our proof:


                                                            7
      Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


378
             • First we determine the convergence behavior of p and v when the norm constraint R is
379            sufficiently large.
380
             • Using properties derived from this convergence, we can analyze the training and test errors.
381
382   The first part of the proof builds upon techniques from Ataee Tarzanagh et al. (2023b), which
383   shows that jointly solving for v and p leads to convergence to their respective max-margin solutions.
384   While their approach focuses on the asymptotic case where R, r → ∞ under specific conditions on
385   the training data, our work extends these techniques to the signal-noise data model and provides
386   non-asymptotic results.
387
      To begin, consider the output of the attention layer ri = Xi⊤ S(Xi p) which is a combination of
388   signal and noise tokens. This can be considered as a “token selection” based on softmax probabilities.
389   Since {ri }i∈[n] determines the model’s output, we prove that only by selecting signal tokens for clean
390   samples and noise tokens for noisy samples can we reach the maximum margin when performing
391   SVM on (ri , yi )i∈[n] and we refer to this as optimal tokens.
392   Definition 12 (Optimal Token). We define the optimal token for sample (Xi , yi ) as
393
                         (1)                                                           (2)
394               ri⋆ = xi     = µk , i ∈ Ck , k ∈ {1, 2}       and            ri⋆ = xi      = ξi , i ∈ N   (4)
395
396   Based on this optimal token selection, we define the corresponding max-margin solution for p and v,
397   denoted by pmm and vmm . We first define pmm as follows:
398   Definition 13 (p-SVM).
399
                                        pmm = argmin ∥p∥        subject to:
400                                               p∈Rd
401
                               p⊤ (µk −ξi ) ≥ 1, i ∈ Ck and p⊤ (ξi − µi ) ≥ 1, i ∈ N
402
403   for all k ∈ {1, 2}, i ∈ [n]. Let Ξ := 1/∥pmm ∥ be the margin induced by pmm .
404
405
      Then for a given p, we define v(p) as the standard max-margin classifier on (ri , yi )i∈[n] and vmm as
406
      the standard max-margin classifier on (ri⋆ , yi )i∈[n] which represents the limiting case when p = pmm
      and R → +∞.
407
408   Definition 14 (v-SVM).
409                            v(p) := argmin ∥v∥ s.t. yi · v ⊤ ri ≥ 1,       for all i ∈ [n].              (5)
410                                      v∈Rd
411   Γ(p) := 1/∥v(p)∥ is the label margin induced by v(p). When ri = ri⋆ , i ∈ [n], we define
412
413                            vmm := argmin ∥v∥ s.t. yi · v ⊤ ri⋆ ≥ 1,       for all i ∈ [n].              (6)
                                         v∈Rd
414
415   Γ := 1/∥vmm ∥ is the label margin induced by vmm .
416
417
      To show the optimality of this token selection, we prove that any other token selection that incorporates
      other tokens in ri will strictly reduce the label margin. This is formalized in the following proposition:
418
419   Proposition 15 (optimal token condition). Suppose that Assumption 5 holds, with probability at least
420   1 − δ over the training dataset, for all p, the token selection under p results in a label margin (Def.
421
      14) of at most Γ − ∥vmmC∥3 nρ2 · max(1 − siαi ) where αi = I(i ∈ C) + 2I(i ∈ N ) and C > 0 is some
                                         i∈[n]
422   constant.
423
424   Then, it is natural to make a conjecture that when jointly optimizing p and v for (2), they will
425
      converge to their respective max-margin solutions pmm and vmm as R, r → ∞. We verify and
      formalize it in the following theorem.
426
427   Theorem 16. Suppose that Assumption 5 holds, with probability at least 1 − δ on the training dataset,
428
      we have
429
             • The margin induced by p(r,R) /R in p-SVM is at least (1 − ζ)Ξ, where
430                                             p
431                                        log(4 ρ2 + (1 + κ)d∥vmm ∥3 dρ2 )
                                      ζ=                                       .
                                                          RΞ

      Accuracy

442
443   Figure 1: The left panel shows the train and test accuracies during training. It shows that benign
444   overfitting occurs after 2 iterations. After the first iteration, the model correctly classifies the clean
445   training examples, but not the noisy ones. In the right panel, we show the softmax probability
446
      of the signal token for clean and noisy samples (average of the softmax probabilities stj,1 over
      C and N respectively). We see that after 2 iterations, the attention focuses on signal tokens for

449

451                     • The label margin induced by v(r,R) /r in v-SVM is at least (1 − γ)Γ, where γ =
                            √
452                        2 ρ2 +(1+κ)d
453                       Γ exp((1−ζ)RΞ) .

454
      Here, (ζ, γ) quantify the difference between (p(r,R) , v(r,R) ) and (pmm , vmm ). As R → ∞, both
455
      ζ and γ converge to 0. Thus, for sufficiently large R, we conclude that p⊤    (r,R) (µk − ξi ) becomes
456
457
      large for i ∈ Ck . This ensures that p(r,R) captures sufficient information about signal tokens, which
      enhances the accuracy of test sample predictions. Specifically, the attention weight on a signal token
458
      is lower bounded by 0.5(1 − ζ)RΞ ≤ ⟨p(r,R) , µj ⟩. Since the signal token remains invariant between
459
      training and test data, we can estimate the attention layer’s output for a new test sample (X, y).
460
461   Lemma 17. Suppose that Assumption 5 holds, with probability at least 1 − δ on the training dataset,
462
      for a given test sample (X, y) with X = (µ⋆ , ξ ⋆ ), where the             ⋆
                                                                         signal µ can⋆be µ1 or µ2 , we  have
                                             1 1
463
      with probability at least 1 − exp − 2 ( 2 (1 − ζ)Ξ − K/R) that ⟨p(r,R) , µ ⟩ − ⟨p(r,R) , ξ ⋆ ⟩ ≥ K,
                                                                     2

464   for K ≤ 12 (1 − ζ)RΞ. Here ζ, Ξ follow the definitions in Theorem 16.
465
      Therefore, if K is large, which is equivalent to R is large, the attention weight on the signal token is
466   much greater than the noise token. As a result, the signal token µ⋆ will dominate the attention layer’s
467   output, i.e. r ⋆ → µ⋆ .
468
469
      Finally, from Theorem 16, v(r,R) converges to vmm , ensuring that it can make accurate predictions on
470
      (µk , y) if (µk , y) comes from the clean set. Thus, w.h.p. the learning of signal token y · ⟨v(r,R) , µ⋆ ⟩
471
      is large enough to eliminate the randomness introduced by the noise token (denoted by ∆(ξ ⋆ ) here)
      and the model will make accurate prediction with high probability: y · f (p(r,R) , v(r,R) ; X) ≈
472
            ⊤
473
      y · v(r,R) µ⋆ − ∆(ξ ⋆ ) ≥ 0.
474
475   6                E XPERIMENTS
476
477   We complement our theoretical results with an empirical study on benign overfitting in single-head
478   softmax attention. We trained single-head softmax attention models (Eq. (1)) on data generated as
479   specified in Section 2.1 using GD with a fixed step size and the logistic loss function. In all figures,
480   the x-axis corresponds to the time and has a log scale. We add 1 to the time so that the initialization
481   t = 0 can be shown in the log scale (i.e. iteration 100 is the initialization).
482   In Figure 1, we consider a setting similar to Theorem 4, and demonstrate that benign overfitting
483   occurs after two iterations, and that the behavior of GD aligns with our discussion in Remark 11. We
484   also plot how the softmax probabilities evolve during training, and see after two iterations a behavior
485   similar to the first item of Theorem 4. In Figure 2, we consider a similar setting, but with a smaller
      step size. Here, benign overfitting occurs after about 150 iterations.


                                                                              9
      Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


486
      In Figure 3a, we explore the behavior of GD with different SNR levels. When the SNR is too small the
487   model exhibits catastrophic overfitting, namely, it fits the training data but has trivial generalization
488   performance. When the SNR is sufficiently large we observe benign overfitting. In Figure 3b, we
489   investigate the overfitting behavior with different dimensions d. If d is sufficiently large we observe
490   benign overfitting. If it is very small we are not able to overfit, namely, the training accuracy does
491   not reach 1. For intermediate values of d we observe harmful overfitting. Thus, we see that high
492   dimensionality is crucial for benign overfitting. Interestingly, we can see that achieving benign
493   overfitting is possible even when d ≪ n2 , suggesting that our assumption on d in the theoretical
494   results might not be tight.

534
535   7                C ONCLUSION
536
537   This paper took an initial step in establishing the benign overfitting phenomenon in a single-head
538   softmax attention model. Our results open up several future directions, including analyzing gradient
539   descent for more than 2 steps, more complex data distributions containing more than 2 tokens and
      varying sequence length, and the self-attention architecture.

672   Remark 18. Throughout our proofs, we assume without loss of generality that µ1 = (ρ, 0, 0, ..., 0)⊤ ,
673   µ2 = (0, ρ, 0, ..., 0)⊤ and ξi = (0, 0, ξ ⊤ ) for ξ ∼ N (0, Id−2 ). Indeed, since µ1 and µ2 are
674   orthogonal, we can find orthogonal matrix A ∈ Rd×d such that Aµ1 = (ρ, 0, 0, ..., 0)⊤ , Aµ2 =
675   (0, ρ, 0, ..., 0)⊤ and Aξi ∼ N (A0, A(Id − µ1 µ⊤        2       ⊤    2   ⊤
                                                         1 /ρ − µ2 µ2 /ρ )A ), which mean that Aξi =
676   (0, 0, ξ ⊤ ) for ξ ∼ N (0, Id−2 ). We emphasize that an orthogonal transformation does not affect our
677   results.

      A.1     P ROOFS FOR S EC . 3
681
682
683   A.1.1     N OTATIONS FOR S EC . 3.
684
685   Given a, b, c ∈ R, we denote by c(a ± b) the close segment [c(a − b), c(a + b)]. Given vector x, we
686   denote by x[i] the ith coordinate of x, and x[i : j] denotes the subvector containing the elements
687   from the ith to the j th , inclusive. We also list some key notations used in this section for convenience.
688
689
690                                          Table 1: Usefull notation.
691

                      γi,j                yi vt⊤ xi,j i.e. j th token score in time t

                      αi,j    softmax probability of the j token in the i sample in time t

699   We remind that C, N ⊆ [n] denotes the indices of clean and noisy training examples, and Ck , Nk
700   denotes the clean and noisy examples from cluster k ∈ {1, 2}. For example if i ∈ C1 , then xi,1 = µ1
701   and y1 = 1, and for j ∈ N1 we have that xj,1 = µ1 and y1 = −1. Let S′ (v) := ∇S(v) =
      diag(S(v)) − S(v)S(v)⊤ denote the Jacobian of the softmax function S(v) at v ∈ Rd .

      Definition 19 (Good Training Set). We say that a training set (X1 , . . . , Xn ) is good if
714
715           • ∥ξi ∥22 ∈ (1 ± on (1))d, for all i ∈ [n].
716                           p
717           • |⟨ξi , ξj ⟩| ≤ d log(12n2 /δ), for any i, j ∈ [n].
718
             • |Nk | ∈ n2 (η ± on (1)) and |Ck | = n2 (1 − η ± on (1)), for k ∈ {1, 2}.
719
720
      Definition 20 (Good Test Sample). We say that a test sample (X = (x1 , x2 ), y) is good w.r.t. a
      training set (X1 , . . . , Xn ) and constant C1 if
721
722                                                           d
                                            |⟨xi,2 , x2 ⟩| ≤      , ∀i ∈ [n]
723                                                          C1 n

      Next we write Lemma 59 slightly different, and also add a formal proof for completeness:
725
726   Lemma 21. Let δ > 0 and C > 0. Suppose that Assumption 23 (item 1) holds with constant C, then
727
      with probability at least 1 − δ/2 we have that
728
                              n          p                n      p
                      |Ck | ∈ (1 − η ± 2/C), |Nk | ∈ (η ± 2/C), ∀k ∈ {1, 2}.
729                           2                           2
730   Moreover, we have
731                            n                          n
                       |Ck | ∈ (1 − η ± on (1)), |Nk | ∈ (η ± on (1)), ∀k ∈ {1, 2} .
732                            2                          2
733
      Proof. By Hoeffding’s inequality,
734                                       n             p
735                           P |Cj | − (1 − η) ≥ n log(16/δ)/2 ≤ δ/8,
736                                        2
737   which means that with probability at least 1 − δ/8 we have that |Cj | ∈ n2 (1 − η ± cn ), where
            p                                                              p                √  p
738   cn = 2n log(16/δ)/n. Hence, if n ≥ C log(16/δ), then cn = 2 log(16/δ)/ n ≤ 2/C.
739   Similarly, we can estimate |Nk | for k ∈ {1, 2}, and by union bound, the result follows.
740   Lemma 22. Let z, γ, p ∈ R2 and let α = S(p), then
741
742                                z T S′ (p)γ = (γ1 − γ2 )(1 − α1 )α1 (z1 − z2 )
743
744   Proof. Observe that α1 + α2 = 1. Therefore,
745                                                               2
                                                                  X                  2
                                                                                     X             2
                                                                                                   X
746                z T S′ (p)γ = z T diag(α)γ − z T αα⊤ γ =             zi αi γi −         αi zi         α i γi
747                                                               i=1                i=1           i=1

748                            = z1 α1 γ1 + z2 α2 γ2 − (α1 z1 + α2 z2 )(α1 γ1 + α2 γ2 )
749                            = (γ2 − (α1 γ1 + α2 γ2 )) α2 z2 + (γ1 − (α1 γ1 + α2 γ2 )) α1 z1
750                            = (α1 γ2 − α1 γ1 ) α2 z2 + (α2 γ1 − α2 γ2 ) α1 z1
751
                               = −α1 (γ1 − γ2 ) α2 z2 + α2 (γ1 − γ2 ) α1 z1
752
                               = α1 (γ1 − γ2 ) α2 (z1 − z2 )
753
754
755
      Lemma 22 allows us to analyze ∇p L as a function of the score gap.

      Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


756
      A.1.3    P ROOF OF T HM . 4
757
758   Proof of Thm. 4. To simplify the proof, we will use the following assumption, which is slightly
759   weaker than Assumption 3:
760                                                          √
761   Assumption 23 (Assumptions for GD with SNR = Θ(1/ n)). Let δ > 0 be a desired probability
762
      of failure. For constants cρ ≥ 6, cβ ≥ 16cρ log(c2ρ ), there exists some large enough constant
763
      C = C(cβ ), such that the following hold:
764
765         1. Number of samples n should be sufficiently large: n ≥ C log(16/δ)
766
            2. Dimension d should be sufficiently large: d ≥ Cn2 log(12n2 /δ).
767
768
                                         p
            3. Signal strength is: ρ = cρ d/n
769
770         4. Label flipping rate η: η ≤ 1/C.
771
            5. The step size β satisfies: β = (cβ · n)/(c2ρ · d).
772
773         6. Initialization at zero: ∥v0 ∥ = ∥p0 ∥ = 0.
774
775
      Apart from slight adjustments to the constants within the logarithm at items 1 and 2 (which can
776
      be absorbed into C), the only changes are cβ ≥ 16cρ log(c2ρ ) (instead of Cβ ≥ 16) and β =
777
      (cβ · n)/(c2ρ · d) (instead of β = Cβ · (n/d)). Indeed, given Cβ ≥ 16, Cρ ≥ 6 and β = Cβ · (n/d)
778
779
      which satisfy Assumption 3, define cρ := Cρ ≥ 6, cβ := Cβ c2ρ ≥ 16cρ log(c2ρ ), which holds for any
780   cρ ≥ 6. We also have that β = Cβ · (n/d) = (cβ /c2ρ ) · (n/d) , i.e., β, cρ , cβ satisfy Assumption 23.
781   Next, under Assumption 23, we argue that with probability at least 1 − δ the training set is good (Def.
782   19) i.e.:
783
784           • |Ck | ∈ n2 (η ± on (1)) and Nk ∈ n2 (1 − η ± on (1)), for k ∈ {1, 2}.
785
786           • ∥ξi ∥22 ∈ (1 ± on (1))d, for any i ∈ [n].
787                           p
788           • |⟨ξi , ξj ⟩| ≤ d log(12n2 /δ), for any i, j ∈ [n].
789
790   Indeed, this holds by Lemma 57, Lemma 21, and the union bound. We emphasize that the notation
791   on (1) represents a term that becomes arbitrarily small as n increases, and thus it can be bounded by a
792   small constant if C from Assumption 1 is large enough.
793   Next, we show that under a good training set, the model exhibits benign overfitting, already after two
794   iterations. See Remark 18 for the data setting used throughout the proof.
795

1198
       A.2.1     A DDITIONAL N OTATION
1199
1200   We first introduce some additional notations. Denote
1201
                         n1 = |C|,    n2 = |N |;     n1i = |Ci |,     n2i = |Ni | for i = 1, 2.
1202
1203   Denote the output of the softmax layer S(Xi p) by
1204
                                                si = (1 − βi , βi )⊤ .
1205
1206   Denote the output of the attention layer Xi⊤ si by ri = (1 − βi )µi + βi ξi , where 0 ≤ βi ≤ 1 is the
1207   attention on the noise token of each sample. Then f (Xi ; p, v) = ⟨v, ri ⟩ can be treated as a linear
1208   classifier on (yi , ri )i∈[n] . Additionally, from the property of log function, item 1 in Assumption 5
1209   can be understood as d ≥ Cn2 log(poly(n)/δ) and the same is for item 5.
1210
1211   A.2.2     P ROOF OF T HM . 6
1212   Proof Sketch
1213   There are two main parts in our proof. In the first part, we prove that only by selecting signal tokens
1214   for clean samples and noise tokens for non-clean samples can we reach the maximum margin when
1215   doing SVM on (yi , ri )i∈[n] .
1216   Definition 24 (Optimal Token). We define the “optimal token" for sample (Xi , yi ) as
1217
1218
                                                   ri⋆ = µi , i ∈ C
1219                                               ri⋆ = ξi , i ∈ N                                      (35)
1220
1221
       Next we define the respective max-margin solution for p and v. We will show that when jointly
       optimizing parameters p and v for (2), they will converge to their respective max-margin solutions as
1222
       R, r → ∞, which are pmm and vmm defined as follows.
1223
1224
       Definition 25. (p-SVM)
                                               pmm = argmin ∥p∥
1225                                                           p
1226   subjected to

1230   for all i ∈ [n]. Ξ = 1/∥pmm ∥ is the margin induced by pmm .
1231
1232   Then for a given p, we define v(p) as the standard max-margin classifier on (yi , ri )i∈[n] and vmm
1233   as the standard max-margin classifier on (yi , ri⋆ )i∈[n] which can be understood as the limit scenario
1234   when p = pmm and R → +∞ .
1235   Definition 26. (v-SVM)

       After proving the converfnece direction of pR and vr , we can utilize their properties similar to pmm

1359   Based on the lemmas above, we introduce our main proposition in this section:
1360   Proposition 15 (optimal token condition). Suppose that Assumption 5 holds, with probability at least
1361   1 − δ over the training dataset, for all p, the token selection under p results in a label margin (Def.
1362   14) of at most Γ − ∥vmmC∥3 nρ2 · max(1 − siαi ) where αi = I(i ∈ C) + 2I(i ∈ N ) and C > 0 is some
                                        i∈[n]
1363   constant.
1364
1365   Proof of Proposition 15. The main idea is to show the optimality of the token selection rule in the
1366   sense that mixing any other tokens will shrink the label margin. For a given p, we say a sample xi is
1367   a “mixed sample” if ri ̸= ri⋆ . We say ri is a mixture of optimal token and non-optimal token in this
1368   case. Note that for any p with finite norm, ri ̸= ri⋆ . This notation is introduced for the clearness of
1369   the proof.
1370   We use contradiction to prove Proposition 15 by showing that any token selection different from (35)
1371   can only result in a strictly smaller label margin than that for the max-margin problem (37). Since v
1372   satisfies the KKT conditions of the max-margin problem, we can write v as
1373                                                      X              X
1374                                v = λ1 µ1 + λ2 µ2 +       yi θi ξi +     yi θ i ξ i .               (43)
                                                         i∈C             i∈N
1375
1376   For a given p, denote v as the max-margin solution in (37), and Γ′ = 1/∥v ′ ∥ as the new label margin.
                              ′

1377   According to Lemma 28, we have
1378
                                                     1     ηn
1379                                 ∥vmm ∥2 = Θ 2 +             = Ω(1/ρ2 ).
1380
                                                    ρ       d
1381   Then we have
1382                                  C                                 C         Γ
                          Γ−                 · max(1 − siαi ) ≥ Γ −             ≥
1383                              ∥vmm ∥3 nρ2 i∈[n]                 ∥vmm ∥3 nρ2   2
1384   for sufficiently large d. Here the last inequality uses ∥vmm ∥2 = Ω(1/ρ2 ). Thus we only need
1385   consider the case when the new label margin Γ′ ≥ Γ/2, or equivalently,
1386
                                                  ∥v ′ ∥ ≤ 2∥vmm ∥.                                       (44)

       Assume that there are k samples (0 < k ≤ n) that violdate the token selection rule (35) and among
1389
       them, p samples are from clean set C and k − p samples are from label-flipped set N . Denote the
1390
       indices of the k samples as Iv . Then we consider the following three scenarios:
1391
1392         1. p ̸= 0, k − p = 0. (All mixed samples come from C)
1393
1394         2. p = 0, k − p ̸= 0. (All mixed samples come from N )
1395
             3. p ̸= 0, k − p ̸= 0. (Mixed samples are from both sets)

       We will separately discuss each scenario and show that Proposition 15 holds in all cases.

       Proof of Lemma 29. Same as Condition 1, Condition 3 does not have any constraint for samples with

1455   Condition 1. Therefore, the bounds established in Lemma 27 remain applicable to {θi′ }i∈N .

2088   Then we consider the other case.
2089
2090   Case 2.2 k − p = n2
2091   In this case, all noisy samples are mixed. From previous analysis, this is equivalent to strengthening
2092   all conditions yi v ⊤ ξi ≥ 1 while other conditions remain the same. As mixing k − p samples will not
2093   result in a better solution than only mixing 1 noisy sample, the proof is the same as Case 2.1 and we
2094   omit it for convenience.
2095
2096   Finally, we consider the last scenario.
2097
2098   Case 3: p ̸= 0, k − p ̸= 0
2099   This scenario is more complex as both clean and noisy sets are mixed. There are four cases to consider
2100
2101         1. p < n1 , k − p < n2 . (Both clean and noisy sets are partially mixed)
2102
2103
             2. p < n1 , k − p = n2 (Clean set is partially mixed, noisy set is all mixed)
2104         3. p = n1 , k − p < n2 (Clean set is all mixed, noisy set is partially mixed)
2105
             4. p = n1 , k − p = n2 (Both clean and noisy sets are all mixed)


                                                             39
       Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


2106
       We will go over every case to prove Proposition 15 holds.
2107
2108   Case 3.1 p < n1 , k − p < n2
2109
2110
       This case is simple because from the analysis above, mixing 1 more clean sample is equivalent to
       adding 1 more constraint and mixing 1 more noisy sample is equivalent to strengthening 1 original
2111
       constraint. So mixing both sets will not result in a better solution than only mixing 1 clean sample.
2112
       Therefore, the proof is the same as Case 1.1 and we omit is for convenience.
2113
2114   Case 3.2 p < n1 , k − p = n2
2115
2116
       In this case, all noisy samples and part of clean samples are mixed. We can consider this case as an
       extension of Case 2.2 by mixing some clean samples. From previous analysis, mixing 1 more clean
2117
       sample is equivalent to adding 1 more constraint. So this case will not result in a better solution than
2118
       Case 2.2. The following proof is the same as Case 2.2 and we omit it for convenience.
2119
2120   Case 3.3 p = n1 , k − p < n2
2121
2122
       In this case, all clean samples and part of noisy samples are mixed. We can consider this case as an
       extension of Case 1.2 by mixing some noisy samples. From previous analysis, mixing 1 more noisy
2123
       sample is equivalent to strengthening 1 original constraint. So this case will not result in a better
2124
       solution than Case 1.2. The following proof is the same as Case 1.2 and we omit it for convenience.
2125
2126   Case 3.4 p = n1 , k − p = n2
2127
2128
       This case is more complex. We cannot simply consider it as an extension of Case 2.2 because the
       analysis of Case 2.2 is based on the condition that there exist clean samples that follow optimal token
2129
       selection rule. Denote ri = βi µi + (1 − βi )ξi for i ∈ C and ri = (1 − βi )µi + βi ξi for i ∈ N . The
2130
       condition in this case becomes

2227
2228   Training and Test Error Analysis
2229   From Proposition 15 we can analyze the properties of both parameters to estimate the training and
2230
       test error.
2231   In this section, we first get the convergence direction of parameters p and v. The main difference
2232   between our setting with Ataee Tarzanagh et al. (2023b) is that they only consider the infinite case and
2233   their results hold only when R, r → ∞. We extend their results to the finite case. Specifically, given
2234   fixed upper bound R and r for ∥p∥ and ∥v∥ respectively, we denote the solution of the constrained
2235   optimization (2) as (vr , pR ) in this section for brevity.
2236   Our main theorem in this section estimates the corresponding deviation of pR /R and vr /r from
2237   their convergence direction pmm /∥pmm ∥ and vmm /∥vmm ∥. For a given p, it is elementary that the
2238   margin induced by p is mini,ti ̸=αi (xiαi − xiti )⊤ p/∥p∥, thus when ∥p∥ = 1, the margin becomes
2239   mini,ti ̸=αi (xiαi − xiti )⊤ p. And for a given v, the label margin induced by v is mini yi v ⊤ ri /∥v∥.
2240   Recall that the label margin induced by vmm is Γ and the margin of p-SVM induced by pmm is Ξ.
2241   First we introduce a lemma to estimate the norm of ∥pmm ∥. This will benefit our proof of the main
2242   theorem.

       token is upper bounded by a small value.

3296   that Theorem 16 does not depend on the selection of optimal tokens, so it still holds in this case when

       Lemma 57. (Properties of Training Data) Suppose that δ > 0 and κ = O( log(6n/δ)/d) =
3493       √
       O(1/

       Proof of Lemma 57. By Bernstein’s inequality (see Theorem 2.8.1 in Vershynin (2018)), with proba-
3500
       bility at least 1 − δ/(3n) we have
3501                                                      p
3502                                    |∥ξi ∥22 − d| = O( d log(6n/δ)).
3503                                  p
       Therefore, there exists κ = O( log(6n/δ)/d) that
3504
3505                                         (1 − κ)d ≤ ∥ξi ∥22 ≤ (1 + κ)d.
3506   Moreover, ⟨ξi , ξj ⟩ has mean zero. For any i, j ∈ [n] and i ̸= j, by Bernstein’s inequality, with
3507   probability at least 1 − δ/(3n2 ) we have
3508                                                      p
                                          |⟨ξi , ξj ⟩| ≤ 2 d log(6n2 /δ).

       Applying a union bound completes the proof.
